        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2239484791" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13891686604111107957">I don't like historical novels<br/><br/>The standard detours from proven history for the sake of sales/unfulfilled romantic fantasies or just sheer laziness drives me nuts and makes me shiver in disgust, yes "reluctant empress" talking to you....<br/><br/>However, occasionally, either because known facts are few and somewhat unreliable or because i love the author, i 'll give it a try and perhaps, just perhaps, once in a blue moon i am pleasantly engaged and surprised.<br/><br/>Ines del Alma Mia (Ines of my soul in the engli</span>
  <span id="freeText13891686604111107957" style="display:none">I don't like historical novels<br /><br />The standard detours from proven history for the sake of sales/unfulfilled romantic fantasies or just sheer laziness drives me nuts and makes me shiver in disgust, yes "reluctant empress" talking to you....<br /><br />However, occasionally, either because known facts are few and somewhat unreliable or because i love the author, i 'll give it a try and perhaps, just perhaps, once in a blue moon i am pleasantly engaged and surprised.<br /><br />Ines del Alma Mia (Ines of my soul in the english version) is one of those exceptions - entirely thanks to the remarkable lady whose history is told and to the literary talent of the wonderful Isabel Allende.<br />This was actually a reread..i cant honestly remember when i first read it, but this time around i wanted to "do it right" and read it in Spanish...yeah literary snob i am, but hey ghosts are allowed their quirky traits aren't we? ;)<br />The story told is that of Ines Suarez, spanish by birth, married in a fit of lust mistaken by love to a "rascal" good with ladies but bad with life in general, and how ines one day decides that she has had enough with life in spain and departs to the new world in search of her husband, him having left a few years earlier in search of riches and fame.<br />At the time i had no idea, but Isabel picked up a known historical figure and her tale of love, death, war, survival and amazing guts that would make her one of the founders of Chile - and let me tell you dear future reader, even if only a third of the story Allende tells is true...my god, what an awesome lady Ines was!<br />There's something to be said about having read this one in spanish, i truly dont think the translation did justice to the emotion that comes off these pages, to the passion that Isabel clearly felt for this particular tale and how well she tells it.<br />The usual Allende marks are here, but what makes this one stand out, as is usually the case with the more exceptional books of Isabel (exceptional at least for me) was the author's particular talent to depict a strong female character...Allende is at her finest when she writes about strong women, (just read La Casa de los Espiritus) and intertwines her own experiences with those of her characters.<br />This book will disturb some more sensitive stomachs, after all Ines is a daughter of her age and was not a saint, there are acts committed that will disgust a modern reader and we are not spared more gruesome descriptions of what happened in South America after the arrival of the Spaniards - however its the truth, why sugar coat it? That being said, this is very much worth a read, if for no other reason than to be immersed in the amazing descriptions of the life and times, and the places throughout which the story moves...its a visual feast in the full sense of the word.<br />Love Allende? you're in for a great read<br />Curious about the times but dont like "traditional" history books? well i can think of much worse ways to be introduced to the beginning of the spanish south american empire!<br />So you don't think i'm a total snob, the english translation is good and does a fairly decent job with the text, just lacks some emotion that only spanish can convey properly i think (no, i'm not a native spanish speaker or of spanish ancestry). <br />Anyways, i ramble so apologies and Happy Readings!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13891686604111107957" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_523404085" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/523404085" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17035182-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359760528p2/17035182.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/523404085?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/17035182-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007241187" href="/book/show/837676.In_s_of_My_Soul">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer523404085" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7472980981104885121">Inés of My Soul is a fantastic book to read if you enjoy fictional literature which also manages to be educational. Although, calling this book 'fictional' is not entirely true, given that Inés Suárez, as well as many of the other characters in the book such as Rodrigo de Quiroga and Pedro de Valdivia, were real, living conquistadors of the 1500's. There is an authors note at the book of the book which states;<br/><br/>"This novel is a work of intuition, but any similarity to events and persons related to</span>
  <span id="freeText7472980981104885121" style="display:none">Inés of My Soul is a fantastic book to read if you enjoy fictional literature which also manages to be educational. Although, calling this book 'fictional' is not entirely true, given that Inés Suárez, as well as many of the other characters in the book such as Rodrigo de Quiroga and Pedro de Valdivia, were real, living conquistadors of the 1500's. There is an authors note at the book of the book which states;<br /><br />"This novel is a work of intuition, but any similarity to events and persons related to the conquest of Chile is not coincidental. The feats of Inés Suárez noted by the chroniclers of her era were nearly ignored by historians for more than four hundred years. In these pages I narrate events as they were documented. My hand merely strung them together with a fine thread of imagination."<br /><br />That little note summaries what this book is really about; Allende has taken the facts and retellings of Inés Suárez and has filled in the blanks using her imagination (or as I've heard her call it; "educated guesses") in order to create this gripping work of fictional-non-fiction. There are some incredibly intense battle scenes and her wonderful use of languages manages to convey the brutal violence of the time, yet somehow doesn't make you recoil in horror. <br /><br />Another Allende book well worth reading. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7472980981104885121" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7185565555285221951">This book did not captivate me the way Allende's other books did. Historically, very little is known about Inés Suarez, the lone woman who left Spain for Peru with the conquistadores in the 1500's, eventually becoming one of the conquerors of Chile and wife of the Royal Governor. Allende did a wonderful job of shaping Inés's voice; transforming her from the almost anonymous historical figure she is into a passionate, admirable woman whose words and actions matter in the grand scheme of things. D</span>
  <span id="freeText7185565555285221951" style="display:none">This book did not captivate me the way Allende's other books did. Historically, very little is known about Inés Suarez, the lone woman who left Spain for Peru with the conquistadores in the 1500's, eventually becoming one of the conquerors of Chile and wife of the Royal Governor. Allende did a wonderful job of shaping Inés's voice; transforming her from the almost anonymous historical figure she is into a passionate, admirable woman whose words and actions matter in the grand scheme of things. Doña Inés played a major role in establishing Santiago, the first Chilean colony, while also ensuring the safety of its people during countless wars and skirmishes with the Mapuche Indians. A large portion of the book is one massacre after another including historically accurate details of the hideous dehumanizing tortures and terrible executions practiced by both the Mapuche and the Spaniards. It grew tedious.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7185565555285221951" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2480284347" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2480284347" />
    <a title="Quinn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5178448-quinn"><img alt="Quinn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476393360p2/5178448.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2480284347?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Quinn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Quinn" href="/user/show/5178448-quinn">Quinn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2480284347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6988237285254142087">Overall Ines of My Soul is a solid read. Even though I had mixed feeling over the course of my reading, I can saw both that I'm glad I got to experience it and I'm also glad to be done reading it. <br/><br/>I was initially intrigued by the premise, I love obscure historical power ladies. And to find out that a 16th century city was founded in part thanks to a woman is even cooler. And what the most interesting part is is how Allende is able to show the slow progression of Ines's change from simple seamstr</span>
  <span id="freeText6988237285254142087" style="display:none">Overall Ines of My Soul is a solid read. Even though I had mixed feeling over the course of my reading, I can saw both that I'm glad I got to experience it and I'm also glad to be done reading it. <br /><br />I was initially intrigued by the premise, I love obscure historical power ladies. And to find out that a 16th century city was founded in part thanks to a woman is even cooler. And what the most interesting part is is how Allende is able to show the slow progression of Ines's change from simple seamstress to gobernador of Chile, through the simple application of hard work and endurance. I enjoyed that for as much history as the novel imparts, it's equal parts romance. I don't know how much of it is extrapolated vs. based in history, but the emphasis on Ines's interaction with her lovers, is a welcome break in the constant travel and daily living. <br /><br />I don't spend much time reading historical fiction so forgive me if some of my issues are more genre specific as opposed to Allende specific. My issues in general stem from the narration of the novel, specifically, Allende's choice to have the story told through the thoughts of elder Ines from her deathbed dictated to her daughter Isabel. It's a storytelling technique that allows the author to break up the flow of the story disguised by the rambling of an aging woman. At first it was hardly noticeable, and for that I am thankful, but by the 6th and final chapter I found the storytelling to be broken and disjointed as Ines jumps about in time. She mentions Pedro's death at multiple intervals only to catapult back to before it occurred multiple times before the actual happenings are revealed in the final pages. <br /><br />What is also jarring is the fact that, up until the final chapter the book is told in a first person narrative, meaning that we only know facts through the eyes of Ines, which explains why we don't get to see all the war efforts that occur during the founding of Chile. It is nice because it means that we only learn information as Ines would, through the word of mouth of a neighbor or through direct interaction with the subject. It allows Allende to create a rich world of constantly changing facts and information which works well in a historical narrative where things are often proven and disproven at a later date. Then all of a sudden, Allende chooses to narrate the rising of Pedro's greatest nemesis, Lautaro, including the trials and tribulations he must overcome to become toqui of his tribe. In other words, Allende all of a sudden switches 5/6ths of the way through to a 3rd person narrative. Although I found the information and his storyline interesting, it is jarring in terms of how we were being fed information up until then, and adds another mark to the disjointedness of the final chapter. <br /><br />In general, I would have enjoyed a lot more dialogue with the story, and again I think this all leads back to the fact that it is written as a reminiscence as opposed to a quest. Sometimes we just go pages and pages with Ines narrating the state of the people or animals, her memories of hungry and humid nights, or telling us about her conversations with others instead of including dialogue. Because of this, a lot of the story comes off as passive, as a list of things that have happened instead of as Ines actually living it. And because of this, I found the story to lack pull. Telling action events in past tense tends to take the excitement of out the situation. I would much rather read them as they are occurring. <br /><br />Even though I focused a lot on my issues, it doesn't change the fact that the subject matter is indeed interesting. I enjoyed the history not just of the conquering Spaniards but of the poor Yanaconas and Mapuche. I just wished she chosen a more interesting way to bestow her wealth of information. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6988237285254142087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Clare O&#39;Beara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clare O&#39;Beara" href="/user/show/23233462-clare-o-beara">Clare O&#39;Beara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23233462-clare-o-beara?shelf=south-america-mexico-historical">south-america-mexico-historical</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer16207108510348597910">This is an astounding book about the establishment of the Spanish state of Chile. There were plenty of other people there, of course, referred to as Indians and consisting of various tribes. None of this mattered to most Spaniards, except for a workforce. <br/><br/>Told from the point of view of Ines, a seamstress from a decent but plain family, who was bored in Spain and followed her conquistador husband to Peru, only to discover that he had been killed, and then made herself useful to the man who was be</span>
  <span id="freeText16207108510348597910" style="display:none">This is an astounding book about the establishment of the Spanish state of Chile. There were plenty of other people there, of course, referred to as Indians and consisting of various tribes. None of this mattered to most Spaniards, except for a workforce. <br /><br />Told from the point of view of Ines, a seamstress from a decent but plain family, who was bored in Spain and followed her conquistador husband to Peru, only to discover that he had been killed, and then made herself useful to the man who was being sent to conquer Chile, the tale reverberates with love, loss and slaughter. <br /><br />The only people the Spanish liked killing better than Indians appears to have been the Spanish. And many of the invaded Indian tribes quite liked killing the Spanish too. Some of them even took lessons from the conquistadors in how to go about it. <br /><br />Dona Ines dictates to a young woman near the end of her life. At the end the author lists her sources (the book is translated from the Spanish, so of course most of the sources are in Spanish) and she comments on how the vigorous, practical, indomitable woman was brushed over in the official accounts, even though her deeds are there in the records; dowsing for water across the desert, founding towns and institutions, cooking, sewing and nursing for armies. All this with the leery eye of the Church upon her, an unmarried woman. <br /><br />This is an adult book due to romance and war; and the racism, genocide and abuses of the period. <br />I read this book from the Royal Dublin Society on the recommendation of the library staff. This is an unbiased review. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16207108510348597910" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2318778117" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14309102179802001406">“Ines…” was one of the novels on the Clear Glade list that I was most looking forward to, as the topic of the Spanish conquest of South America really interests me. It is the story of Ines Suarez, a Spanish woman of modest origins who rose to become governor of the kingdom of Chile. The story is told by Ines, in her own words, but features many famous cameos- e.g., Pizarro, de Valdivia, etc.<br/><br/>The novel does a good job of trying to tell the story of both sides. I’ll never take the part of the conqu</span>
  <span id="freeText14309102179802001406" style="display:none">“Ines…” was one of the novels on the Clear Glade list that I was most looking forward to, as the topic of the Spanish conquest of South America really interests me. It is the story of Ines Suarez, a Spanish woman of modest origins who rose to become governor of the kingdom of Chile. The story is told by Ines, in her own words, but features many famous cameos- e.g., Pizarro, de Valdivia, etc.<br /><br />The novel does a good job of trying to tell the story of both sides. I’ll never take the part of the conquistadors, however, Allende does make efforts to tell both sides of the story, and to paint some positive traits onto the conquistadors.<br /><br />The novel also features a lot of blood and gore and tragedy…(expected, I suppose, for a novel about war and genocide).<br /><br />There’s not much suspense for the most part of the novel, given that it is a work of historical fiction, and most of us already know what happened to many of the main players. However, I found the last part of the novel quite suspenseful and absorbing.<br /><br />This was a good, straight-forward novel for historical fiction buffs. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14309102179802001406" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9567701919087034674">History is more amazing than any fantasy portrayed on tv. This book taught me more about the conquest of South America than a history class I took in college. I only gave this novel 3 stars because at times the novel was hard to follow and went back and forth in time with a confusing number of names. The story though was impressive especially what Inés accomplished in the 16th century. It would be impressive for a man but ten times that because she was a woman and had so many constraints put on </span>
  <span id="freeText9567701919087034674" style="display:none">History is more amazing than any fantasy portrayed on tv. This book taught me more about the conquest of South America than a history class I took in college. I only gave this novel 3 stars because at times the novel was hard to follow and went back and forth in time with a confusing number of names. The story though was impressive especially what Inés accomplished in the 16th century. It would be impressive for a man but ten times that because she was a woman and had so many constraints put on her. What I’m left with is just how awful men can be in the name of war, conquest, riches and power. Women truly are the reason our race is still alive and even to this day I wish women were given the credit they deserve. What the Europeans did in the name of religion was horrific. </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2657799972">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3233526319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16939982494066104081">a rare 5 stars from this picky reader<br/>Lush, richly detailed, immerses one quickly into the era and the characters. The accuracy of the historical events provides all the plot points one could desire. More would be hyperbole. No need for tacky ' plot points ' so beloved of commercialized authors these days. The history is sufficiently interesting --- if not, go watch tv! <br/><br/>It seems that every 20th/21st century author of South American extraction must throw in politics and a bit of magical realism; t</span>
  <span id="freeText16939982494066104081" style="display:none">a rare 5 stars from this picky reader<br />Lush, richly detailed, immerses one quickly into the era and the characters. The accuracy of the historical events provides all the plot points one could desire. More would be hyperbole. No need for tacky ' plot points ' so beloved of commercialized authors these days. The history is sufficiently interesting --- if not, go watch tv! <br /><br />It seems that every 20th/21st century author of South American extraction must throw in politics and a bit of magical realism; the winning formula for Garcia Marques. Allende does so, but gracefully and not from a bully pulpit or virtue-signalling. The conquest of what is now called Peru and Chile was brutal and she minces little, rather is accurate. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16939982494066104081" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VdSeH7uNTWiQydMuPuDiJU0KHXrYvWyhwoXwr8ymRkEBZ4WJ+4BEAPI5Q3h4HF3159+jk3ua3aO5TzusNotMpQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('owEm/E04SiMdTrH1sDIM2+xv5GVYhZPF6r7InMsujDj3sj1qDTVDS3++IaP2zrMLRrpajPuiIseRdAOfMQOG3A==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

En un vedado estado independiente enclavado entre las cumbres heladas de los Himalayas, la tarea del equipo consiste en localizar a su legendario Dragon de Oro, una estatua sagrada y un oraculo invaluable que puede predecir el futuro del reino. En su lucha para llegar a la estatua antes de que sea destruida por la avaricia de un intruso, Alexander y Nadia deben usar el poder trascendente de los espiritus de animales totemicos, Jaguar y Aguila. Con la ayuda de un sabio monje budista, su joven discipulo de la realeza, y una feroz tribu de guerreros Yeti, Alexander y Nadia luchan para proteger la sagrada doctrina del Dragon de The survivor of a troubled childhood, he has taken refuge in the world of books and spends his nights spinning baroque tales about the city’s underworld. But perhaps his dark imaginings are not as strange as they seem, for in a locked room deep within the house lie photographs and letters hinting at the mysterious death of the previous owner. \n\nLike a slow poison, the history of the place seeps into his bones as he struggles with an impossible love. Close to despair, David receives a letter from a reclusive French editor, Andreas Corelli, who makes him the offer of a lifetime. He is to write a book unlike anything that has ever existed — a book with the power to change hearts and minds. In return, he will receive a fortune, and perhaps more. But as David begins the work, he realizes that there is a connection between his haunting book and the shadows that surround his home.\n\nOnce again, Zafon takes us into a dark, gothic universe first seen in The Shadow of the Wind and creates a breathtaking adventure of intrigue, romance, and tragedy. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9607890878566397292">Isabel Allende Llona is a Chilean-American novelist. Allende, who writes in the "magic realism" tradition, is considered one of the first successful women novelists in Latin America. She has written novels based in part on her own experiences, often focusing on the experiences of women, weaving myth and realism together. She has lectured and done extensive book tours and has taught literature at s</span>
  <span id="freeText9607890878566397292" style="display:none">Isabel Allende Llona is a Chilean-American novelist. Allende, who writes in the "magic realism" tradition, is considered one of the first successful women novelists in Latin America. She has written novels based in part on her own experiences, often focusing on the experiences of women, weaving myth and realism together. She has lectured and done extensive book tours and has taught literature at several US colleges. She currently resides in California with her husband. Allende adopted U.S. citizenship in 2003.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9607890878566397292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
